<div id="descriptionContainer">
      <div style="right:0" class="readable stacked" id="description">
        
<span id="freeTextContainer12776921234580120779">A wizard predicts his own death and readies himself for the traditional transfer of power to the eighth son of an eighth son. The snag is that the eighth son is a daughter and women can't be wizards.</span>

      </div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_61529998">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/61529998" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/922495-patrick" class="left imgcol" title="Patrick"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1351307095p2/922495.jpg" alt="Patrick" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/61529998?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 15, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/922495-patrick" class="user" title="Patrick">Patrick</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/34507.Equal_Rites" title="Paperback 9780060855901" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer61529998">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14650970232662919042">I just recently re-visited this book after a couple years away from it. What's more, I've just recent re-read several of the more recent Witch novels from Pratchett, so they're fresh in my head. <br /><br />Granny Weatherwax is one of my favorite characters of Pratchett's, and as an author, it does me good to see how she began as a character. <br /><br />This book has some rough edges. There's nothing wrong with it, mind you, but it was still very early on in Pratchett's career, and it doesn't have the smoothness of hi</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14650970232662919042">I just recently re-visited this book after a couple years away from it. What's more, I've just recent re-read several of the more recent Witch novels from Pratchett, so they're fresh in my head. <br /><br />Granny Weatherwax is one of my favorite characters of Pratchett's, and as an author, it does me good to see how she began as a character. <br /><br />This book has some rough edges. There's nothing wrong with it, mind you, but it was still very early on in Pratchett's career, and it doesn't have the smoothness of his later work. Discworld is not nearly as developed, and neither is his writing style. <br /><br />Granny doesn't have her friend Nanny Ogg as conversational foil and counterpoint in this book, and it's surprising how much that limits her character. What's more, while you can see elements of the character Granny eventually becomes, there's a surprising streak of country bumpkin in here here. In later books she loses most of that (which is for the best) and while she may not be worldly, she is still self-posessed and wise. <br /><br />Another interesting echo is the relationship between Granny and Esk. Twenty years later, Pratchett brought a similar relationship to beautiful fruition with Tiffany Aching. <br /><br />As a result, this book is merely great rather than utterly brilliant. Even rough-hewn early Pratchett is better than 75% of all books out there.<br /><br />As a side note, this is not a bad entry point into reading Discworld. Normally I advise people begin at the beginning of the series, but despite this being the third book of Discworld, it makes for a better start than either of the first two books.... <br /><br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14650970232662919042">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_45541981">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/45541981" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1713956-manny" class="left imgcol" title="Manny"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442491692p2/1713956.jpg" alt="Manny" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/45541981?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 28, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1713956-manny" class="user" title="Manny">Manny</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/34507.Equal_Rites" title="Paperback 9780060855901" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer45541981">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2235523805813619822">The problem with Terry Pratchett is that you keep wanting to read the good bits out loud. <br /><br />In this particular case, I'd just reached the line "Her dress would have been both clinging and revealing, if it had had anything to cling to or reveal." Too late, I realized that not all the people around me were going to find this equally funny. I'm still embarrassed. Damn.<br /><br /></span>

          </span>
        </div>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/331108824?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 20, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5293536-melki" class="user" title="Melki">Melki</a>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=humorous-fiction" class="actionLinkLite">humorous-fiction</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/5293536-melki?shelf=wascally-witches" class="actionLinkLite">wascally-witches</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer331108824">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15282037285661538370">Similar in spirit to the first two books in the Discworld series, once again we have a delightful duo on a journey, encountering many a merry mishap on the way. This book is not as funny as its predecessors, though the plot seems more cohesive and a little less meandering. <br /><br />Despite the distinct lack of trolls, this is probably my favorite so far. I really enjoyed the "Girl Power" theme to the book. At least I think I did. It could just be those darned witches using their "headology" on me.<br /></span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1190015046">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1190015046" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/10743961-rob" class="left imgcol" title="Rob"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1422270416p2/10743961.jpg" alt="Rob" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1190015046?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 29, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/10743961-rob" class="user" title="Rob">Rob</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/6567830-equal-rites" title="Kindle Edition " class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/10743961-rob?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/10743961-rob?shelf=humor" class="actionLinkLite">humor</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/10743961-rob?shelf=discworld" class="actionLinkLite">discworld</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/10743961-rob?shelf=read-2015" class="actionLinkLite">read-2015</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/10743961-rob?shelf=own" class="actionLinkLite">own</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1190015046">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15498226372154393184"><strong>Executive Summary:</strong> Not as funny or as quotable as <a title="The Light Fantastic (Discworld, #2; Rincewind #2) by Terry Pratchett" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34506.The_Light_Fantastic__Discworld___2__Rincewind__2_">The Light Fantastic</a>, but very enjoyable for other reasons.<br /><br /><strong> <u>Full Review</u> </strong><br />I had to double check the year this was written. This book still feels very relevant today.<br /><br />Wizards can only be men. Witches can only be women. Their magic is different and shouldn't be mixed. A women has no place learning to be a wizard. Witches "have their place". Does any of this sound familiar?<br /><br />As someone who works in a field that is far too lacking in women the idea that ce</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15498226372154393184"><strong>Executive Summary:</strong> Not as funny or as quotable as <a title="The Light Fantastic (Discworld, #2; Rincewind #2) by Terry Pratchett" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34506.The_Light_Fantastic__Discworld___2__Rincewind__2_">The Light Fantastic</a>, but very enjoyable for other reasons.<br /><br /><strong>
  <u>Full Review</u>
</strong><br />I had to double check the year this was written. This book still feels very relevant today.<br /><br />Wizards can only be men. Witches can only be women. Their magic is different and shouldn't be mixed. A women has no place learning to be a wizard. Witches "have their place". Does any of this sound familiar?<br /><br />As someone who works in a field that is far too lacking in women the idea that certain disciplines are more suited for men or women is still a stigma we seem to be fighting today.<br /><br />That isn't to say this book is preachy or in your face about it. It simply that the satire is definitely more directed at real world issues than fantasy tropes like the first two books. It sounds like this sort of thing is more common in later books, so I find it interesting that he changed up the style so early on in the series.<br /><br />And while it wasn't quite as funny to me as <a title="The Light Fantastic (Discworld, #2; Rincewind #2) by Terry Pratchett" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34506.The_Light_Fantastic__Discworld___2__Rincewind__2_">The Light Fantastic</a>, there were more than a few laugh out loud moments and quotes that I highlighted for later.<br /><br />Plus, Granny Weatherwax is a great character. I've read that she changes quite a bit in the <a title="Wyrd Sisters (Discworld, #6; Witches #2) by Terry Pratchett" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34504.Wyrd_Sisters__Discworld___6__Witches__2_">Wyrd Sisters</a> and beyond, but I'm glad I can see how she started out before I jump into that.<br /><br />Overall this book had to do something right, because I pretty much tore through it in a weekend, which despite it's short length is still rather fast for me.<br /><br />I already jumped right into <a title="Mort (Discworld, #4; Death, #1) by Terry Pratchett" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/386372.Mort__Discworld___4__Death___1_">Mort</a> as Discworld seems to have its hooks into me right now.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="15498226372154393184">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1180344210">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14414347904125230395">If I was not already a Terry Pratchet fan, I most definitely am now.<br /><br />Equal Rites, Sir Terry’s third installment in the Discworld series is a peach of practical magic. Telling the story of a young girl’s conflicting talents for wizardry and / or witchery.<br /><br />In the Discworld, men are wizards and women are witches – at least that is how it has been up to the point when young Eskarina Smith sort of becomes – both. Pratchett spins a deliciously tangled web about the age-old contest between the men and th</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText14414347904125230395">If I was not already a Terry Pratchet fan, I most definitely am now.<br /><br />Equal Rites, Sir Terry’s third installment in the Discworld series is a peach of practical magic. Telling the story of a young girl’s conflicting talents for wizardry and / or witchery.<br /><br />In the Discworld, men are wizards and women are witches – at least that is how it has been up to the point when young Eskarina Smith sort of becomes – both. Pratchett spins a deliciously tangled web about the age-old contest between the men and the ladies. <br /><br />“I’m not a lady, I’m a witch,” said Granny. <br /><br />Eskarina may be the protagonist but there is no doubt that Granny Weatherwax stole the show. Filling the witch role in the small village of Bad Ass and always appearing in serviceable black, Granny kicks ass and takes names throughout the fun narrative. I am very pleased to learn that my favorite witch will make many more appearances in Pratchett’s series – nine more to be sure.<br /><br />A good witching time atop Great A'Tuin, Equal Rites is the best one I have read so far.<br /><br /><img width="400" height="400" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1422661899i/13531848.jpg" /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="14414347904125230395">...more</a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/928709048?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 05, 2014</a>

          added it


          <span class="greyText"> · </span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer928709048">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4301544972428449002"><strong>Fun reading</strong><br /><br />This is book one of the Witches segment of Discworld. The characters are lively and likable. The magic system is comedic with a dark bite. Mixed into the slapstick silliness is a grain of philosophy and social commentary that is often highly quotable and thought provoking.<br />The story reads like Wicca meets Harry Potter meets the theory of relativity meets The Dark Crystal. I found myself slowing down and rereading sections of the story to make sure I followed it correctly. A lot happens</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText4301544972428449002"><strong>Fun reading</strong><br /><br />This is book one of the Witches segment of Discworld. The characters are lively and likable. The magic system is comedic with a dark bite. Mixed into the slapstick silliness is a grain of philosophy and social commentary that is often highly quotable and thought provoking.<br />The story reads like Wicca meets Harry Potter meets the theory of relativity meets The Dark Crystal. I found myself slowing down and rereading sections of the story to make sure I followed it correctly. A lot happens in a short period of time in this story, and at times I felt like I missed something.<br /> I read the first Rincewind prior to this, and had heard the Witches storyline was better. This is only the second discworld book I have read. I do like the Witches story slightly better, but Rincewind was good also. I plan to read both and work my way through this series. <br />For years, I put off reading these because it didn't really seem like the type of story I would enjoy. I'm glad, because now I have them all ahead of me to read this summer. They make nice, comedic filler between my more serious reads.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="4301544972428449002">...more</a>

          </span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_723011272">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/723011272" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/7438487-olga-godim" class="left imgcol" title="Olga Godim"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1442639299p2/7438487.jpg" alt="Olga Godim" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/723011272?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Dec 04, 2013</a>

      <a href="/user/show/7438487-olga-godim" class="user" title="Olga Godim">Olga Godim</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/7438487-olga-godim?shelf=fantasy-scifi" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy-scifi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer723011272">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6081283831437718760">A mediocre novel, at least for this writer. He’s still stretching his wings, and it shows: this earlier tale contains too much verbal clutter but almost no humor, which is abundant in his later novels. I like the idea of this one – a female should be allowed to be a wizard. Oh, yeah, I’m all for equal rights. I dislike the execution though. <br />Why did the author make Esk, the protagonist, an 8-year-old girl? She is too young to behave the way she does and to know everything she is supposed to know.</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6081283831437718760">A mediocre novel, at least for this writer. He’s still stretching his wings, and it shows: this earlier tale contains too much verbal clutter but almost no humor, which is abundant in his later novels. I like the idea of this one – a female should be allowed to be a wizard. Oh, yeah, I’m all for equal rights. I dislike the execution though. <br />Why did the author make Esk, the protagonist, an 8-year-old girl? She is too young to behave the way she does and to know everything she is supposed to know. She should’ve been at least 15. The book definitely targets adult readers, so such a young protagonist doesn’t make sense. <br />On the other hand, Granny Weatherwax, the second protagonist and Esk’s mentor, is her usual grumpy and resourceful self, a champion of headology, which is a philosophy invented by Pratchett (I think). I like Granny’s lectures to Esk: <blockquote>
  <strong>“Listen,” said Granny. “If you give someone a bottle of red jollop for their wind it may work, right, but if you want it to work for sure then you let their mind <em>make</em> it work for them. Tell ’hem it’s moonbeams bottled in fairy wine or something. Mumble over it a bit…”</strong>
</blockquote> Despite her formidable magical abilities, Granny uses headology most of the time, and it always works for her and her clients. She is one of the best literary witches of the genre, and the more I read about her the more I like her. <br />Overall impression: for Pratchett’s purist – a necessary read. For the rest – you can skip it. There are much better books in the Discworld series. <br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6081283831437718760">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <a href="/book/show/3129541-ritos-iguales" title="Paperback 9788497930208" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1343968928">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11679868454380719242">Tercera novela de Mundodisco y la primera de la saga de las Brujas. En esta ocasión un mago moribundo cede su cayado mágico a un bebe recién nacido, Eskarina Herrero, una octava hija de una octavo hijo. El problema es que la magia de magos solo pasa de magos a niños; y las niñas en cambio solamente pueden ser brujas, ya que su magia es totalmente distinta. A pesar de sus peros, la bruja Yaya Ceravieja decide velar por Esk, ayudarla a convertirse en bruja y también en mago; llevándola hasta la Un</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText11679868454380719242">Tercera novela de Mundodisco y la primera de la saga de las Brujas. En esta ocasión un mago moribundo cede su cayado mágico a un bebe recién nacido, Eskarina Herrero, una octava hija de una octavo hijo. El problema es que la magia de magos solo pasa de magos a niños; y las niñas en cambio solamente pueden ser brujas, ya que su magia es totalmente distinta. A pesar de sus peros, la bruja Yaya Ceravieja decide velar por Esk, ayudarla a convertirse en bruja y también en mago; llevándola hasta la Universidad Invisible para completar su formación.<br />Como siempre ha sido un relato ameno, ligero, cortito, con sus aventuras absurdas y diálogos desternillantes. Pero esta vez me ha gustado más, ha sido un relato que no se ha ido tanto por las ramas y que deja una crítica social a la orden del día, el machismo. Sin perder el sentido del humor, nos presenta el símil de que solo se puede ser mago o bruja según tu género con el mundo laboral de la actualidad.<br />Los personajes son geniales, sobre todo los dos principales. La pequeña Esk me gusta cómo va cambiando y evolucionando, pero es que Yaya Ceravieja se come las paginas, cada dialogo de ella yo no podía parar de reírme, es un personaje único que le da vida a esta novela. Además. Creo que de Yaya tendremos más aventuras a lo largo de la saga, pero que de Esk hasta la saga de Tiffany Dolorido no tendremos ninguna noticia. Que ganas tengo de continuar con la saga de Las brujas, siempre me pasa que termino cada libro, próxima parada, la maravillosa Mort.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="11679868454380719242">...more</a>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_382065818">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/382065818" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies" class="left imgcol" title="David Sarkies"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1445680853p2/5856247.jpg" alt="David Sarkies" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/382065818?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 14, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5856247-david-sarkies" class="user" title="David Sarkies">David Sarkies</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/833422.Equal_Rites" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780552131056" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Anybody who likes a good laugh</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to David by: </span>
      Stewart
  </div>



          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5856247-david-sarkies?shelf=comedy" class="actionLinkLite">comedy</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer382065818">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3627219549617345684"><strong>Granny takes on the old boys club</strong><br />4 August 2012<br /><br /> There are a few things that I have to say before commenting on this book as such. Firstly this is the second time that I read it, but I have listed it as a read book because when I read it the first time the friend who had lent it to me then proceeded to tell me all of the jokes. In fact, every Discworld novel that he ended up encouraging me to read generally came with a running commentary, and as such I ended up getting put off of them quite quickl</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3627219549617345684"><strong>Granny takes on the old boys club</strong><br />4 August 2012<br /><br />	There are a few things that I have to say before commenting on this book as such. Firstly this is the second time that I read it, but I have listed it as a read book because when I read it the first time the friend who had lent it to me then proceeded to tell me all of the jokes. In fact, every Discworld novel that he ended up encouraging me to read generally came with a running commentary, and as such I ended up getting put off of them quite quickly. As it turned out, the funniest Discworld novel that I read was <a title="Guards! Guards! (Discworld, #8; City Watch #1) by Terry Pratchett" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/64216.Guards__Guards___Discworld___8__City_Watch__1_">Guards Guards</a> because that was the one novel that I read before he could spoil it for me. Secondly, I must say that I prefer the covers drawn by Paul Kirby as opposed to the other covers that I have looked at on the Goodreads site because his art seems to capture the essence of Discworld.<br /><br />	I decided that instead of writing commentaries on the Discworld books that I read a while back that I would actually give Pratchett a chance by re-reading them and I must say that I am really glad that I did. His use of the English language is little short of masterful, which is not surprising since prior to becoming a full time author he was a journalist. While anybody who knows how to construct a proper sentence can write a story, it takes a lot of skill to write a story the way Pratchett does. Not only does he use puns in a way that actually make them funny (with the title being an example) but he is also able to use modern pop-culture to describe aspects of the Discworld in a way that actually does not make me cringe. While I do not believe that he is mixing metaphors, it is not exactly something that I have seem any fantasy writers really do. However not only does Pratchett do it and do it well, he also gets away with it.<br /><br />	Now, this book is about how a young girl inherits, quite by accident, a wizard's staff, and in inheriting the staff she also inherits the wizard's magical ability. The catch is that on Discworld women are not supposed to be wizards. It is not that they can't be wizards, it is just that they are not supposed to be wizards (it is sort of like an old boy's club). It harkens back to the early twentieth century when women were not supposed to do a lot of things, not that they couldn't, but because the male dominated society said that they were not allowed. Obviously things had changed a lot by the time that Pratchett wrote this book, but in a way he showing us how the idea that women should not do a man's job is nothing short of absurd. However, I do suggest that things have gone a bit too much in the opposite direction, namely because some women are being allowed to work in areas simply because they are women, not because they are actually good at I. Please don't get me wrong, I am all in favour is gender equality and smashing open the old boys networks, it is just that I prefer appointments based on merit as opposed to gender. It is like the days when a noble would command an army, not because the noble could actually command an army (and in many cases they couldn't) but because they were a noble. The whole idea to me is absurd. I guess that is why Pratchett wrote this book.<br /><br />	I have suggested that this was probably Pratchett's make or break book, namely because he had already completed the two part <a title="The Colour of Magic (Discworld, #1) by Terry Pratchett" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/601238.The_Colour_of_Magic__Discworld___1_">Colour of Magic</a> and <a title="The Light Fantastic (Discworld, #2; Rincewind #2) by Terry Pratchett" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/34506.The_Light_Fantastic__Discworld___2__Rincewind__2_">Light Fantastic</a>, and here he appears to have pretty much dropped the original characters and started afresh with new characters: Granny Weatherwax and Esk (though Esk hasn't appeared any of the books since – or at least the ones that I have read). Granny will appear in other books down the track, but she in introduced here, with her powerful stare and her headology (the art of making people think something when it really is not the case). However it has been suggested to me that this story was not well received. Personally I cannot say but since he has written 32 novels, I guess this book did make him, though I also suspect that by the time we reach book 20 we find that only die hard Pratchett fans are buying his books (in the same way that die hard King fans buy Stephen King books). I still note that the odd Pratchett book or five seem to be commonplace on many a bookshelf.<br /><br />	Finally I would suggest that Pratchett is not necessarily doing anything new, but rather taking an old style and putting it into a fantasy novel. His clever use of metaphors remind me of <a title="Douglas Adams" href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/4.Douglas_Adams">Douglas Adams</a> (the Vogon Constructor fleet hung in the sky the way bricks don't) however from what I remember of Douglas Adams, his Hitch-hikers series seems to hit a brick wall at <a title="Life, the Universe and Everything (Hitchhiker's Guide, #3) by Douglas Adams" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/8694.Life__the_Universe_and_Everything__Hitchhiker_s_Guide___3_">Life the Universe and Everything</a>. Secondly, the story reminded me of the Asterix books, though instead of creating a comic he writes a story. However the way he writes the story, particularly when Esk meets the wolves in the forest, puts pictures of Asterix comics in my head.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="3627219549617345684">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer3203860589262649948">Como me viene pasando siempre con las novelas de Pratchett, esta es una historia que se disfruta muchísimo ya no tanto por la trama sino por la ambientación única y el estilo divertidísimo e inigualable del autor. Por algo será que, de julio a ahora, he pasado de no haber leído ninguna novela suya a haber leído cuatro. En Mundodisco, Pratchett se salta todas las reglas de la coherencia narrativa; hace lo que le da la gana... y le sale bien. Pues chapó.<br />En concreto, de ritos iguales me llevo unas</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText3203860589262649948">Como me viene pasando siempre con las novelas de Pratchett, esta es una historia que se disfruta muchísimo ya no tanto por la trama sino por la ambientación única y el estilo divertidísimo e inigualable del autor. Por algo será que, de julio a ahora, he pasado de no haber leído ninguna novela suya a haber leído cuatro. En Mundodisco, Pratchett se salta todas las reglas de la coherencia narrativa; hace lo que le da la gana... y le sale bien. Pues chapó.<br />En concreto, de ritos iguales me llevo unas protagonistas entrañables y divertidas, y algunos conceptos de lo más interesantes, más allá de la comedia con la que están casi maquillados. Sí, «cabezología», y tal.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1278295484">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1278295484" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/19126246-kaitlin" class="left imgcol" title="Kaitlin"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1449671391p2/19126246.jpg" alt="Kaitlin" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1278295484?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 16, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/19126246-kaitlin" class="user" title="Kaitlin">Kaitlin</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/20493914-equal-rites" title="Hardcover 9781473200203" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1278295484">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9894800669466565719">This is book 3 (publication order) of the Discworld books and after having read and enjoyed both The Colour of Magic and The Light Fantastic last year I was quite looking forward to getting into some of the books which major fans of the series say are good. I can definitely say that this one is a lot better then The Colour of Magic in both writing style and ease of understanding. By this point it seems the Pratchett had really honed and perfected his tone of voice and writing style, just the rig</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9894800669466565719">This is book 3 (publication order) of the Discworld books and after having read and enjoyed both The Colour of Magic and The Light Fantastic last year I was quite looking forward to getting into some of the books which major fans of the series say are good. I can definitely say that this one is a lot better then The Colour of Magic in both writing style and ease of understanding. By this point it seems the Pratchett had really honed and perfected his tone of voice and writing style, just the right amount of sark-y meets funny meets thoughtful.<br /><br />This is book 1 of the Witches series (which is a series within the overall Discworld series) and it's a wonderfully fun beginning to what no doubt promises to be a fun journey. We get to meet Granny Weatherwax who is a Witch and is firmly set in her ways and ideas, at least until something comes along to throw a spanner right in the works - Esk. Granny is a great character with a lot of charm which more than makes up for her abrupt and somewhat dismissive attitude. She's the sort of person I think everyone has maybe met in real life before, she likes to believe she's right and she loves to be respected. Whenever she's wrong, she's not actually wrong, there must be some error along the way. She's also very proficient as a witch unlike some others we encounter in the book. Overall she's a pretty likeable and slightly intimidating character. <br /><br />The other main character we meet is actually the one that the story really revolves around. Her name is Esk and she is the 8th son of an 8th son (but she's not... she's a girl). When a wizard comes looking for the 8th son of an 8th son in order to pass on his magic and staff he doesn't quite realise the mistake he's made until he's long gone and the wizard magic lies dormant within Esk. Esk is a young child, aged 8 when the story really begins, and she is a very inquisitive and actually very bright young child. She likes to think that she knows a lot, but she's always ready to learn more and she definitely does so through the story. I found her to be instantly fun to read about, and her story just got better as it went.<br /><br />One element I absolutely adore from Pratchett is his ability to make heavy or thoughtful topics a central theme or part of the book without it bringing down the tone in any way. When you read his books it always feels easy and fun, and there's never a dull moment of a lull in the writing because it's written to be a bonkers world that will entertain you. It's a fabulous concept and I have to say nothing makes me happier than knowing that I still have over 35+ books left in the series. I can't wait!<br /><br />A solid 4*s - really readable and fun, and highly recommended!</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9894800669466565719">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer168812651">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15014633772479520284"><strong>A brave move from Terry Pratchett as he moves away from his established characters and takes a shot at world building.</strong><br /><br />I've been listening to the audiobook for the reread of this one as part of my exercise regime and it was quite the good distraction from the pain.<br /><br />The third in the now long running Discworld series moves away from Rincewind, The Luggage, Twoflower and the parodies of generic sword and magic fantasy epics. After the success of the first two I imagine this must have been a brave mov</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText15014633772479520284"><strong>A brave move from Terry Pratchett as he moves away from his established characters and takes a shot at world building.</strong><br /><br />I've been listening to the audiobook for the reread of this one as part of my exercise regime and it was quite the good distraction from the pain.<br /><br />The third in the now long running Discworld series moves away from Rincewind, The Luggage, Twoflower and the parodies of generic sword and magic fantasy epics. After the success of the first two I imagine this must have been a brave move for a young author. Seems to have paid off for him in the long run. I can only review from the point of view of having read all of the series at least but I believe as a standalone novel this one was good fun. Looking at it as a building block for what was to come it rates highly too but there are too many discrepencies in the rules of his world and his characters for me to love it in retrospect.<br /><br />Esk is a girl, she's destined to become the first female wizard after a mix up with checking the sex of a baby and a dying wizards last wish. But first she must overcome the ingrained magical misogyny that has become second nature on the Disc. For this she acquires the aid of Granny Weatherwax and embarks on a quest to change the world.<br /><br />In true early Pratchett style silliness abounds, humourous asides are plenty and a start is made on intelligently unravelling human foibles and frailties that would later become his hallmark. If you're reading these for the first time in order you won't notice everything that I had a problem with - Granny Weatherwax isn't as she would later come to be known, she is merely a tool of the author for example, so I'm sure you will enjoy this a lot more than I did this time around because a novice Pratchett is still heads and shoulders above almost everybody else.<br /><br />Next up is Mort, my first ever Discworld read and I'm quite excited at the prospect of running 5km to it. (Mental groan.)<br /><br />My reread of the Discworld can be followed in the following places:<br /><a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/168812577">The Colour of Magic</a><br /><a href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/168812598">The Light Fantastic</a></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_1233419227">

  <a href="/user/show/9077850-molly" class="left imgcol" title="Molly"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1345305363p2/9077850.jpg" alt="Molly" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1233419227?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">May 08, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/9077850-molly" class="user" title="Molly">Molly</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing">it was amazing</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="it was amazing"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/833422.Equal_Rites" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780552131056" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/9077850-molly?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9077850-molly?shelf=terry-pratchett" class="actionLinkLite">terry-pratchett</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9077850-molly?shelf=humor" class="actionLinkLite">humor</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/9077850-molly?shelf=magic" class="actionLinkLite">magic</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1233419227">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18233821751973258187">First in the Witches series, and another great Discworld story.<br /><br />Drum Billet, a wizard knocks on the door of Smith the smithy of Bad Ass, a small village up in the Ramtops. The smithy's wife is about to give birth to her eighth child and the wizard wishes to transfer his powers (before parting with his life) as tradition demands, to the eighth son of an eighth son. Little did he know that the new addition to the smithy's family was gonna be a girl... because you know, only men can be wizards, and</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18233821751973258187">First in the Witches series, and another great Discworld story.<br /><br />Drum Billet, a wizard knocks on the door of Smith the smithy of Bad Ass, a small village up in the Ramtops. The smithy's wife is about to give birth to her eighth child and the wizard wishes to transfer his powers (before parting with his life) as tradition demands, to the eighth son of an eighth son. Little did he know that the new addition to the smithy's family was gonna be a girl... because you know, only men can be wizards, and women ... well ... ahem ... they can become witches and tell fortunes, gather herbs, and assist mothers at birth, if you get the hint. Anyway it is too late for the wizard to undo the transfer when he understands his blunder, because Death is already tapping his shoulder.<br /><br />Well, you can stuff your backward thinking where the sun don't shine, little Eskarina Smith doesn't care what anyone thinks. As soon as she discovers her destiny she leaves the Ramtops with the reluctant Granny Weatherwax, the local witch and heads towards the distant Ankh-Morpork (Discworld's largest city) and the Unseen University to become a wizard.<br /><br />While I understand why some people didn't like Equal Rites after reading the first two books ... personally, I loved it. The book had a different flair than its predecessors (and the City Watch books I read before) but it was delightful. Maybe a story where a little girl and an old witch are the main characters is not for everyone's taste, but come on...<br /><br />If you like to smirk and laugh with Pratchett's Discworld characters give it a chance. You will not be disappointed.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_158006116">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/158006116" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/891206-leslie" class="left imgcol" title="Leslie"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1301450802p2/891206.jpg" alt="Leslie" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/158006116?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Mar 31, 2011</a>

      <a href="/user/show/891206-leslie" class="user" title="Leslie">Leslie</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>



        


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer158006116">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer286535825865915841"><em>Wyrd Sisters</em> was my first Pratchett, and such a bliss-out that I am forever partial to any Discworld narrative that involves witches. <em>Especially</em> if the witch in question is Esmerelda "Granny" Weatherwax. And Granny is front and center in <em>Equal Rites</em>.<br /><br />I won't go into details of the plot except to say that the punny title alludes to the (in this case) magical battle between the sexes which provides much of the story's conflict. See, women can be witches and men can be wizards, but you <em>absolutely ca</em></span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText286535825865915841"><em>Wyrd Sisters</em> was my first Pratchett, and such a bliss-out that I am forever partial to any Discworld narrative that involves witches. <em>Especially</em> if the witch in question is Esmerelda "Granny" Weatherwax. And Granny is front and center in <em>Equal Rites</em>.<br /><br />I won't go into details of the plot except to say that the punny title alludes to the (in this case) magical battle between the sexes which provides much of the story's conflict. See, women can be witches and men can be wizards, but you <em>absolutely cannot</em> have a female wizard or a male witch (warlocks notwithstanding). "The lore," everyone insists, "is against it."<br /><br />Pratchett plays all kinds of games with notions of feminine and masculine temperaments; witches are all about botanicals, the magic of the natural world and psychology. They are also, by-and-large, practically illiterate, and fine with that. Wizards, on the other hand, like numbers and ideas, and most of their magic comes from "words." Scary ones. Trapped in books in whose presence one must be very, very careful.<br /><br />And, of course, while witches learn witchcraft at the be-warted knee of canny women in the woods, wizards go to universities and give themselves grand-sounding but ultimately meaningless titles.<br /><br />While much fun is poked at both sides of the gender gap, Pratchett -- diplomatic as always -- saves his keenest barbs for the boys-club intellectualism of academia. People who get huffy at humor that draws heavily from generalizations about gender may take issue with this book on principle, but my guess is that the prose is charming enough disarm all but the bristliest reader.<br /><br />Postscript -- two other important points to make: (1) I know a lot of words. But I had to go to the dictionary twice ("grimoire" and "incunable") on one page, and was glad I did. (2) Like most Pratchett novels, this is primarily comic. But the beautiful bits are <em>really beautiful</em> and the scary bits are <em>really scary</em></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="286535825865915841">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/232542814" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/5106028-robyn" class="left imgcol" title="Robyn"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1441402727p2/5106028.jpg" alt="Robyn" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/232542814?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jul 05, 2012</a>

      <a href="/user/show/5106028-robyn" class="user" title="Robyn">Robyn</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/34507.Equal_Rites" title="Paperback 9780060855901" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/5106028-robyn?shelf=reviewed" class="actionLinkLite">reviewed</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer232542814">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer18377049398705362295">This is where we really start to see the real meat of the Discworld series, and the wonders to come. <br /><br />It's easy to read the first two books of the series as an exercise in pure iconoclasm. Pratchett came onto the scene, a gleeful jester in the genre, poking fun at the tropes, breaking them down and reassembling them minus a few screws, so that they did something a bit unexpected. With this book however, he lets us know that he's not just doing it for the sake of tinkering with the status quo, he'</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText18377049398705362295">This is where we really start to see the real meat of the Discworld series, and the wonders to come. <br /><br />It's easy to read the first two books of the series as an exercise in pure iconoclasm. Pratchett came onto the scene, a gleeful jester in the genre, poking fun at the tropes, breaking them down and reassembling them minus a few screws, so that they did something a bit unexpected. With this book however, he lets us know that he's not just doing it for the sake of tinkering with the status quo, he's doing it because he has a message. <br /><br />Is it intentional that the message of the story and the effect it had were one and the same? I think so, and it would be hard to persuade me otherwise; I have an uncommon amount of faith in Sir Terry's wiliness. <br /><br />Esk's journey is Terry's journey, plain and simple. They both saw an institution of culture that was being choked with pointless convention, an institution with established rules that strangled and thwarted newcomers, that had become set in it's ways and stagnant. In both cases this institution was founded around a wild, creative impulse that no one rightly understands, yet insists on making rules for it, and dictating to others how it is to be done. Both of them had an inborn talent, which needed expressing, but the rules weren't allowing it. <br /><br />And what is Terry saying here? He's not declaring war, he's not waiting outside the gate of Literature, heavily armed to give it a bonk on the head and some veiled threats. He's simply saying "This is what I do, and I'm going to do it regardless of your rules. Make room for me, and you'll be fine. Maybe something great will come of it, who knows?"<br /><br />From this, he's also making a statement, giving advice to anyone who is stuck in a mire of constricting rules and presuppositions. Even though I feel like what he's saying is also a very personal statement, there are many situations in which this advice is fairly useful. <br /><br />Of course, this is still a Terry Pratchett book, which means plenty of laughs, plenty of adventure, and a surprising amount of brain food from something that seems so light and capricious. It's a fun book on it's own, but taken into the mythology of the whole, it's something more. It's a declaration of intent, done with eloquence, grace and style. </span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="18377049398705362295">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1239894368">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1239894368" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/776624-daniel-attack-of-the-books-burton" class="left imgcol" title="Daniel (Attack of the Books!) Burton"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1450247755p2/776624.jpg" alt="Daniel (Attack of the Books!) Burton" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1239894368?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Jun 10, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/776624-daniel-attack-of-the-books-burton" class="user" title="Daniel (Attack of the Books!) Burton">Daniel (Attack of the Books!) Burton</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/34507.Equal_Rites" title="Paperback 9780060855901" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/776624-daniel-attack-of-the-books-burton?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/776624-daniel-attack-of-the-books-burton?shelf=satire" class="actionLinkLite">satire</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/776624-daniel-attack-of-the-books-burton?shelf=discworld" class="actionLinkLite">discworld</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1239894368">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer5657794243304155557">What more can be possibly said about the late and great Terry Pratchett? I've yet to open a book bearing his name that I do not like, that does not amuse and delight, and does not leave me thoughtful and wiser.<br /><br />Well, wiser at least in my own estimation. I'm sure Pratchett would have something to say about the narcissism of the self-assessment.<br /><br />To Equal Rites itself: Eskarina was supposed to be born the eighth son of an eighth son, an auspicious combination that a dying wizard seeks out in order to</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText5657794243304155557">What more can be possibly said about the late and great Terry Pratchett? I've yet to open a book bearing his name that I do not like, that does not amuse and delight, and does not leave me thoughtful and wiser.<br /><br />Well, wiser at least in my own estimation. I'm sure Pratchett would have something to say about the narcissism of the self-assessment.<br /><br />To Equal Rites itself: Eskarina was supposed to be born the eighth son of an eighth son, an auspicious combination that a dying wizard seeks out in order to pass along his powers. Pass them he does, as Esk is born, but Esk is a girl, not a boy, and she enters the world without regard for the expectations of men and wizards. And indeed, she aims to grow into her power without regard for wizard and men. As her<br /><br />In a day and age when social justice messages of equality and diversity seem to win awards and accolades, Pratchett is a breath of fresh air. Using fantasy, satire and deft wordsmithing, he conveys minute truths and observations that are far more persuasive than heavy-handed and stilted stories to those who have eyes to see, but doesn't bowl over the less interested reader with contrived sermons and meaning.<br /><br />Equal Rites is rife this light-hearted but insightful satire. Esk determines that she must become a wizard, not realizing that the world of magic is divided along gender lines (wizards are men, witches are women). She thrusts herself forward with all the courage and naivete of a child unafraid to ask "why" to the assumptions adults take for granted. It provides an easy foil for the pretensions of stuffy old men and conventions, to question why things are done the way they are done.<br /><br />Pratchett's characters can are fully formed, fully realized, and every subtle description fills in the depth of their character. This starts from his hook as the dying wizard stumbles along towards Esk's home and continues with Granny Weatherwax and her penchant for wearing her entire wardrobe at once. Some books rely on a single hook to pull you in, but Pratchett continues to hook in each chapter, page and paragraph with characters that fly from the page more colorful than real life.<br /><br />While Equal Rites is the third published book from Discworld, it's also the first of the Witches.</span>

          </span>

              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer979031150">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12230466778279820981">The thing I really noticed this time is the way Pratchett makes up for his earlier omission of women at Unseen University by acknowledging how many it takes to keep the university running. And I continue to love how Granny Weatherwax supports herself in Anhk-Morpork and how she gets around the wizards. That Esk is nine doesn't surprise me: she's definitely a predecessor of Tiffany in the competence department. I'd really love to see Esk again, more grown-up, more used to being a first and only.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1080399693">

  <a href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl" class="left imgcol" title="Tadiana ? Night Owl?"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1383845644p2/8734459.jpg" alt="Tadiana ? Night Owl?" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1080399693?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Oct 16, 2014</a>

      <a href="/user/show/8734459-tadiana-night-owl" class="user" title="Tadiana ? Night Owl?">Tadiana ? Night Owl?</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>



        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/8734459-tadiana-night-owl?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1080399693">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2289675879972553592">I've had the first Discworld book patiently waiting on my iPad for months and haven't gotten to it, but per Deborah's <u>strong</u> recommendation (see her review and Emma Sea's comment #1 at <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1062882955?book_show_action=false&amp;page=1" target="_blank">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a>) I skipped past the first two books and went straight to the third, Equal Rites, to begin my Discworld experience.<br /><br />A dying wizard passes his wizardly powers and magical staff to the newborn 8th son of an 8th son (why not 7th? I guess Discworld just has to be different). He d</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText2289675879972553592">I've had the first Discworld book patiently waiting on my iPad for months and haven't gotten to it, but per Deborah's <u>strong</u> recommendation (see her review and Emma Sea's comment #1 at <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1062882955?book_show_action=false&amp;page=1" target="_blank">https://www.goodreads.com/review/show...</a>) I skipped past the first two books and went straight to the third, Equal Rites, to begin my Discworld experience.<br /><br />A dying wizard passes his wizardly powers and magical staff to the newborn 8th son of an 8th son (why not 7th? I guess Discworld just has to be different). He doesn't realize, however (until after he's dead), that the 8th son is a girl, and in this world women are <em>never</em> wizards--only witches. However, the wizardly powers will not be denied, and as Eskarina, or Esk, begins to grow up she has to deal with learning to control her powers while also dealing with a wizardly establishment that refuses to recognize or train girl wizards. Luckily Esk has Granny Weatherwax to help her.<blockquote>[Granny] stood up. "Let's find this Great Hall, then. No time to waste."<br /><br />"Um, women aren't allowed in," said Esk.<br /><br />Granny stopped in the doorway. Her shoulders rose. She turned around slowly.<br /><br />"<em>What</em> did you say?" she said. "Did these old ears deceive me, and don't say they did because they didn't."<br /><br />"Sorry," said Esk. "Force of habit."<br /><br />"I can see you've been getting some ideas below your station," said Granny coldly.</blockquote>This was a fun reading experience, not terribly deep but good for laughs, a lot like <a title="The Hitchhiker's Guide to the Galaxy (Hitchhiker's Guide to the Galaxy, #1) by Douglas Adams" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/11.The_Hitchhiker_s_Guide_to_the_Galaxy__Hitchhiker_s_Guide_to_the_Galaxy___1_">The Hitchhiker's Guide to the Galaxy</a>, except in a fantasy setting rather than science fiction. Humorous asides and tongue-in-cheek humor abound.<blockquote>Bits of gnolls hung from the nearby rocks, giving them a sort of jolly, festive air. Gander wasn't particularly sorry about that--gnolls liked to capture travelers and practice hospitality of the red-hot-knife-and-bludgeon kind . . . Still, it was a relief to get away from that macabre sight. Gander considered that gnolls didn't look any better inside than out. He hated their guts.</blockquote><br />I would have liked a little more substance with my humor, but this was rewarding enough that I'll try dipping my toes into the Discworld series a little further. Maybe I'll jump straight to <a title="Guards! Guards! (Discworld, #8) by Terry Pratchett" href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/64216.Guards__Guards___Discworld___8_">Guards! Guards!</a> and see what the fuss is about Vimes. :)<br /><br />3.5 stars. Thanks again to Deborah for egging me on!<br /><br /><img width="300" height="225" class="escapedImg" alt="description" src="http://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1413503112i/11522146._SX540_.jpg" /><br /><br />Content advisory: Some humorous sexual innuendos. Maybe not for younger teens.</span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_427574227">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/427574227" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3344685-sarah" class="left imgcol" title="Sarah"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1388694025p2/3344685.jpg" alt="Sarah" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/427574227?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Nov 12, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3344685-sarah" class="user" title="Sarah">Sarah</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/833422.Equal_Rites" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780552131056" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/3344685-sarah?shelf=books-i-own-print" class="actionLinkLite">books-i-own-print</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3344685-sarah?shelf=books-i-own-kindle" class="actionLinkLite">books-i-own-kindle</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3344685-sarah?shelf=read-before-2010" class="actionLinkLite">read-before-2010</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3344685-sarah?shelf=british-author" class="actionLinkLite">british-author</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/3344685-sarah?shelf=read-in-2015" class="actionLinkLite">read-in-2015</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer427574227">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6024280313264774909">Wizards always know when they're going to die and Drum Billet is determined to pass on his staff, and his magic, to someone worthy. He has heard that a baby is about to be born, the eighth son of an eighth son, and therefore someone who should be incredibly powerful when they come of age and he has decided that they'll make a worthy heir. Unfortunately he forgets one tiny matter - to check that the baby is actually a boy before he makes the exchange. It's a well known fact on Discworld that girl</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText6024280313264774909">Wizards always know when they're going to die and Drum Billet is determined to pass on his staff, and his magic, to someone worthy. He has heard that a baby is about to be born, the eighth son of an eighth son, and therefore someone who should be incredibly powerful when they come of age and he has decided that they'll make a worthy heir. Unfortunately he forgets one tiny matter - to check that the baby is actually a boy before he makes the exchange. It's a well known fact on Discworld that girls can't be wizards and boys can't be witches but Esk has been gifted a magic that she never should have had access to and now it's up to Granny Weatherwax to figure out how to train her.<br /><br />I've always loved the Discworld witches and Granny Weatherwax is a firm favourite but I had forgotten how much she changes throughout this series. When we meet her in <strong>Equal Rites</strong> she is still an intelligent and powerful witch but she's never left her remote village in the mountains and hates the idea of even visiting a large town, let alone travelling the 500 miles to Ankh-Morpork. I always think of her as being rather worldy and wise so it was quite amusing to see her so nervous about leaving her home. Unfortunately for Granny Esk starts to manifest magical abilities that are beyond Granny's experience and she realises that the young girl needs proper wizard training so it's off to the Unseen University that they go.<br /><br />One of the things I love about <strong>Terry Pratchett's</strong> writing is his ability to take real life issues, like equality of the sexes in this case, and present them in a way that shows how utterly ridiculous it is that these problems still occur in our day to day lives. His stories are so full of humour, he is great at poking fun of old fashioned and pointless traditions and his characters are always so much fun to read about. <strong>Equal Rites</strong> is the third book in the Discworld series but it stands the test of time and I've read it many times over the years along with all of the earlier Discworld novels. I love the world he has created and I've grown really attached to all of the characters over the years, so many of them pop up time and time again even if it's only for a minor guest appearance and although the books can be read in any order I do think that it's best to start at the beginning, especially your first time through the series.</span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="6024280313264774909">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review nosyndicate" id="review_72093815">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/72093815" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/1760824-graham" class="left imgcol" title="Graham"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1380743323p2/1760824.jpg" alt="Graham" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/72093815?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Sep 22, 2009</a>

      <a href="/user/show/1760824-graham" class="user" title="Graham">Graham</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it">liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="liked it"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText"> · </span>
          <a href="/book/show/833422.Equal_Rites" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780552131056" class="lightGreyText">review of another edition</a>


        


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a href="/review/list/1760824-graham?shelf=fantasy" class="actionLinkLite">fantasy</a>, 
              <a href="/review/list/1760824-graham?shelf=humour" class="actionLinkLite">humour</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer72093815">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9889462935164399858">Pratchett’s third Discworld novel dispels with Rincewind and the various other assorted characters we met in the first two books, instead introducing one of the series’ most memorable characters: Granny Weatherwax, the sharp-tongued witch. Unfortunately, Granny is embroiled in a rather slow-moving tale that doesn’t really go anywhere for a hundred pages, before finishing with a rip-roaring conclusion that’s full of amusement, excitement and excellent writing. It’s just a shame that Pratchett did</span>
  <span style="display:none" id="freeText9889462935164399858">Pratchett’s third Discworld novel dispels with Rincewind and the various other assorted characters we met in the first two books, instead introducing one of the series’ most memorable characters: Granny Weatherwax, the sharp-tongued witch. Unfortunately, Granny is embroiled in a rather slow-moving tale that doesn’t really go anywhere for a hundred pages, before finishing with a rip-roaring conclusion that’s full of amusement, excitement and excellent writing. It’s just a shame that Pratchett didn’t go there sooner…<br /><br />The plot is about a little girl who wants to be a wizard, something previously unheard of. Pratchett gets plenty of mileage from this plotline, and there’s fun coming from the possessed wooden staff, which is kind of like the Luggage, in that it has a mind of its own, but not quite as funny or intriguing. When the plot gets moving away from Granny’s hovel, it gets pretty interesting, and there are lots of odd characters and weird situations to enjoy. But I loved Rincewind and his exploits, and I was sorry to see the back of them, and the first half of this books feels like Pratchett treading water. You read it for all the jokes and humour – spot on as usual – but that’s it…<br /></span>
  <a onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;" href="#" data-text-id="9889462935164399858">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars"><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it">really liked it</span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p10" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span><span class="staticStar p0" size="15x15" title="really liked it"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span style="" class="readable" id="reviewTextContainer1188462629">
            
<span id="freeTextContainer13658204626366837749"><strong> <em> "'Million-to-one chances,' she said, 'crop up nine times out of ten.'"</em> </strong><br /><br />This wasn't nearly as funny or nearly as engaging as the first two novels, and that may be due to the absence of the original cast of characters. However, Eskarina, the first female wizard, and her old Granny, a witch, were still entertaining and enjoyable. My only complaint is that this book seems a bit discontinuous from the previous one.<br /><br /></span>

<div itemtype="http://schema.org/Review" itemscope="" itemprop="reviews" class="review" id="review_1239254441">
  <link href="http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1239254441" itemprop="url" />
  <a href="/user/show/3392444-terpkristin" class="left imgcol" title="terpkristin"><img src="http://d.gr-assets.com/users/1438465987p2/3392444.jpg" alt="terpkristin" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a href="/review/show/1239254441?book_show_action=true" itemprop="publishDate" class="reviewDate createdAt right">Apr 17, 2015</a>

      <a href="/user/show/3392444-terpkristin" class="user" title="terpkristin">terpkristin</a>

            Shelves:
